in service to our duty to the Constitution and to our country, the House Committee on the Judiciary is introducing two articles of impeachment, charging the president of the United States, Donald J. Trump, with committing high crimes and misdemeanors. The first article is for abuse of power. It is an impeachable offense for the president to exercise the powers of his public office to obtain an improper personal benefit while ignoring or injuring the national interest. That is exactly what President Trump did when he solicited and pressured Ukraine to interfere in our 2020 presidential election, thus damaging our national security, undermining the integrity of the next election, and violating his oath to the American people. These actions moreover are consistent with President Trump’s previous invitations of foreign interference in our 2016 presidential election. And when he was caught, when the House investigated and opened an impeachment inquiry, President Trump engaged in unprecedented, categorical and indiscriminate defiance of the impeachment inquiry. This gives rise to the second article of impeachment, for obstruction of Congress. Here too, we see a familiar pattern in President Trump’s misconduct. A president who declares himself above accountability, above the American people and above Congress’s power of impeachment, which is meant to protect against threats to our democratic institutions, is a president who sees himself as above the law.” “The evidence of the president’s misconduct is overwhelming and uncontested. And how could it not be, when the president’s own words on July 25, ‘I would like you to do us a favor though,’ lay so bare his intentions, his willingness to sacrifice the national security for his own personal interests? And when the president got caught, he committed his second impeachable act, obstruction of Congress, of the very ability to make sure that no one is above the law, not even the president of the United States. The evidence is every bit as strong that President Trump has obstructed Congress fully without precedent and without basis in law. If allowed to stand, it would decimate Congress’s ability to conduct oversight of this president or any other in the future, leaving this president and those who follow to be free to be as corrupt, malfeasant or incompetent as they would like, with no prospect of discovery or accountability.”"/><div class="css-bsn42l"><span class="css-1dv1kvn">Video</span><div><div class="css-1t0v8sy etkm6pg0"><div class="css-mm3pwi"><meta itemType="transcript" data="“Today, in service to our duty to the Constitution and to our country, the House Committee on the Judiciary is introducing two articles of impeachment, charging the president of the United States, Donald J. These actions moreover are consistent with President Trump’s previous invitations of foreign interference in our 2016 presidential election. And when he was caught, when the House investigated and opened an impeachment inquiry, President Trump engaged in unprecedented, categorical and indiscriminate defiance of the impeachment inquiry. This gives rise to the second article of impeachment, for obstruction of Congress. Here too, we see a familiar pattern in President Trump’s misconduct. A president who declares himself above accountability, above the American people and above Congress’s power of impeachment, which is meant to protect against threats to our democratic institutions, is a president who sees himself as above the law.” “The evidence of the president’s misconduct is overwhelming and uncontested. And how could it not be, when the president’s own words on July 25, ‘I would like you to do us a favor though,’ lay so bare his intentions, his willingness to sacrifice the national security for his own personal interests? And when the president got caught, he committed his second impeachable act, obstruction of Congress, of the very ability to make sure that no one is above the law, not even the president of the United States. The evidence is every bit as strong that President Trump has obstructed Congress fully without precedent and without basis in law. If allowed to stand, it would decimate Congress’s ability to conduct oversight of this president or any other in the future, leaving this president and those who follow to be free to be as corrupt, malfeasant or incompetent as they would like, with no prospect of discovery or accountability.”"/><div class="css-1g7y0i5 e1drnplw0"><button tabindex="100" class="css-1rtlxy" type="button" aria-labelledby="modal-title" role="region"><header class="css-jx2kos"><div class="css-mln36k" id="modal-title">transcript</div><button type="button" class="css-18p0uq6"><div class="css-pbq7ev"></div><span>Back</span></button><div class="css-f6lhej"><div class="css-1ialerq"><button tabindex="99" type="button" class="css-1eut9gw" aria-label="play"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="20" height="20" viewBox="0 0 20 20" fill="none"><path fill-rule="evenodd" clip-rule="evenodd" d="M8 13.7683V6L14.5 9.88415L8 13.7683Z" fill="#333333"></path><circle cx="10" cy="10" r="9.25" stroke="#333333" stroke-width="1.5"></circle></svg></button><div class="css-1701swk"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" viewBox="0 0 40 36" width="6" height="36" id="el_dS5TKNZZ5w"></rect></g></g></g></svg></div><div><div class="css-1t7yl1y">0:00<!-- -->/<!-- -->2:59</div><div class="css-og85jy">-<!-- -->2:59</div></div></div></div></header><div class="css-15fbio0"><div class="css-1p4nyns"><p class="css-1qi8px4">transcript</p><h2 class="css-9wqu2x">Democrats Announce Two Articles of Impeachment</h2><h4 class="css-qsd3hm">Democratic leaders in the House announced that they would move ahead with two articles of impeachment charging President Trump with abuse of power and obstruction of Congress.</h4></div><dl class="css-p98d0w"><dt class="css-xx7kwh"></dt><dd class="css-12tddc8"><p class="css-8hvvyd">“Today, in service to our duty to the Constitution and to our country, the House Committee on the Judiciary is introducing two articles of impeachment, charging the president of the United States, Donald J. Trump, with committing high crimes and misdemeanors. The first article is for abuse of power. It is an impeachable offense for the president to exercise the powers of his public office to obtain an improper personal benefit while ignoring or injuring the national interest. That is exactly what President Trump did when he solicited and pressured Ukraine to interfere in our 2020 presidential election, thus damaging our national security, undermining the integrity of the next election, and violating his oath to the American people. These actions moreover are consistent with President Trump’s previous invitations of foreign interference in our 2016 presidential election. And when he was caught, when the House investigated and opened an impeachment inquiry, President Trump engaged in unprecedented, categorical and indiscriminate defiance of the impeachment inquiry. This gives rise to the second article of impeachment, for obstruction of Congress. Here too, we see a familiar pattern in President Trump’s misconduct. A president who declares himself above accountability, above the American people and above Congress’s power of impeachment, which is meant to protect against threats to our democratic institutions, is a president who sees himself as above the law.” “The evidence of the president’s misconduct is overwhelming and uncontested. And how could it not be, when the president’s own words on July 25, ‘I would like you to do us a favor though,’ lay so bare his intentions, his willingness to sacrifice the national security for his own personal interests? And when the president got caught, he committed his second impeachable act, obstruction of Congress, of the very ability to make sure that no one is above the law, not even the president of the United States. The evidence is every bit as strong that President Trump has obstructed Congress fully without precedent and without basis in law. If allowed to stand, it would decimate Congress’s ability to conduct oversight of this president or any other in the future, leaving this president and those who follow to be free to be as corrupt, malfeasant or incompetent as they would like, with no prospect of discovery or accountability.”</p></dd></dl></div></div></div></div></div><div class="css-eidn6d etkm6pg1"><div class="css-1ihorw etkm6pg2"><img src="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/12/10/us/politics/10dc-impeach-sub2/10dc-impeach-sub2-videoSixteenByNine3000-v2.jpg" alt="Video player loading"/></div><div class="css-1ruigs3"><div class="css-v15h5m"><div></div><div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><figcaption itemProp="caption description name" class="css-17ai7jg emkp2hg0"><span class="css-16f3y1r e13ogyst0">Democratic leaders in the House announced that they would move ahead with two articles of impeachment charging President Trump with abuse of power and obstruction of Congress.</span><span class="css-cch8ym"><span class="css-1dv1kvn">Credit</span><span itemProp="copyrightHolder" class="emkp2hg2 css-1fxp258 e1z0qqy90"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Credit...</span><span>T.J. Kirkpatrick for The New York Times</span></span></span></figcaption></figure><div class="css-18e8msd epjyd6m0"><div class="css-vp77d3 epjyd6m2"><div class="css-hus3qt ey68jwv0" aria-hidden="true"><a href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/nicholas-fandos" class="css-uwwqev"><img alt="Nicholas Fandos" title="Nicholas Fandos" src="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/11/06/multimedia/author-nicholas-fandos/author-nicholas-fandos-thumbLarge-v2.png" class="css-1rjmmt7 ey68jwv2"/></a></div><div class="css-1baulvz"><p class="css-1fv7b6t e1jsehar1" itemProp="author" itemscope="" itemType="http://schema.org/Person">By<!-- --> <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/nicholas-fandos" class="css-brehiz e1jsehar0"><span class="css-1baulvz last-byline" itemProp="name">Nicholas Fandos</span></a></p></div></div><ul class="css-1xtbm1r epjyd6m3"><li class="css-i49r68"><div class="css-ld3wwf e16638kd1"><time dateTime="2019-12-10T08:51:48-05:00" class="css-1sbuyqj e16638kd4">Dec. 10, 2019</time><time dateTime="2019-12-10T10:23:35-05:00" class="css-15rjoqi e16638kd5">Updated <span class="css-epvm6">10:23 a.m. ET</span></time></div></li><li class="css-153c5xt epjyd6m1"><div class="css-4xjgmj"><div role="toolbar" aria-label="Social Media Share buttons, Save button, and Comments Panel with current comment count" class="css-pvvomx" data-testid="share-tools"><ul class="css-y8aj3r"><li class="css-60hakz fb-share-item"><a href="https://www.facebook.com/dialog/feed?app_id=9869919170&amp;link=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.nytimes.com%2F2019%2F12%2F10%2Fus%2Fpolitics%2Ftrump-impeachment-articles.html&amp;smid=fb-share&amp;name=House%20Democrats%20Unveil%20Articles%20of%20Impeachment%20Against%20Trump&amp;redirect_uri=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.facebook.com%2F" class="css-60hakz"><a href="mailto:?subject=NYTimes.com%3A%20House%20Democrats%20Unveil%20Articles%20of%20Impeachment%20Against%20Trump&amp;body=From%20The%20New%20York%20Times%3A%0A%0AHouse%20Democrats%20Unveil%20Articles%20of%20Impeachment%20Against%20Trump%0A%0ADemocratic%20leaders%20announced%20they%20would%20move%20to%20charge%20President%20Trump%20with%20abuse%20of%20power%20and%20obstruction%20of%20Congress%20when%20the%20Judiciary%20Committee%20meets%20to%20consider%20articles%20of%20impeachment%0A%0Ahttps%3A%2F%2Fwww.nytimes.com%2F2019%2F12%2F10%2Fus%2Fpolitics%2Ftrump-impeachment-articles.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer" aria-label="Email"><svg viewBox="0 0 15 9" class="css-4brsb6" width="15" height="9"><path fill-rule="evenodd" clip-rule="evenodd" d="M.906 8.418V0L5.64 4.76.906 8.419zm13 0L9.174 4.761 13.906 0v8.418zM7.407 6.539l-1.13-1.137L.907 9h13l-5.37-3.598-1.13 1.137zM1.297 0h12.22l-6.11 5.095L1.297 0z" fill="#000"></path></svg></a></li><li class="css-60hakz"><div class="css-6n7j50"><button aria-haspopup="true" aria-expanded="false" aria-label="More sharing options ..." class="css-16ogagc" data-testid=""><svg class="css-uhuo44" viewBox="0 0 16 13" width="16" height="13"><path fill-rule="evenodd" clip-rule="evenodd" d="M15.406 5.359L8.978 0v3.215C3.82 3.215.406 8.107.406 12.66 1.653 9.133 4.29 7.517 8.978 7.517v3.2l6.428-5.358z" fill="#000"></path></svg></button></div></li><li class="css-60hakz"></li><li class="css-l72opv"></li></ul></div></div></li></ul></div></header></div><section name="articleBody" itemProp="articleBody" class="meteredContent css-1r7ky0e"><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">WASHINGTON — House Democrats announced on Tuesday that they would move ahead this week with two articles of impeachment charging President Trump with abuse of power and obstruction of Congress, as they accused him of violating the Constitution by pressuring Ukraine for help in the 2020 election. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Speaking from a wood-paneled reception room just off the floor of the House, Speaker Nancy Pelosi and leaders of six key committees said that Mr. Trump’s actions toward Ukraine, and his efforts to block Congress’s attempt to investigate, had left them no choice but to pursue one of the Constitution’s gravest remedies. The move will bring a sitting president to the brink of <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/news-event/trump-impeachment" title="">impeachment</a> for only the fourth time in American history.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“Today, in service to our duty to the Constitution, and to our country, the House Committee on Judiciary is introducing two articles of impeachment charging the president of the United States, Donald J. Trump, with committing high crimes and misdemeanors,” said Representative Jerrold Nadler, Democrat of New York and the panel’s chairman. He stood before four American flags and a portrait of George Washington.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“Our president holds the ultimate public trust,” Mr. Nadler said. “When he betrays that trust and puts himself before country, he endangers the Constitution, he endangers our democracy, and he endangers our national security.”</p></div><aside class="css-ew4tgv" aria-hidden="true"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The announcement comes a day after <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/12/09/us/politics/impeachment-hearings.html" title="">Democrats summed up the central allegations</a> in their impeachment case against Mr. Trump: that he pressured Ukraine to announce investigations into his political rivals while withholding as leverage a coveted White House meeting for its president and $391 million in critical security assistance. His actions, they argued in a lengthy hearing at the Judiciary Committee, had placed the president’s personal political interests above those of the country, threatening the integrity of the election and national security in the process. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">After more than <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2019/10/04/us/politics/president-trump-impeachment-inquiry.html" title="">two months of investigating</a> the Ukraine matter, and a year of confrontation between the Democratic House and Mr. Trump, the impeachment process is now likely to unfold quickly. The Judiciary Committee plans to promptly begin debating the articles as soon as Wednesday, and could vote by Thursday to recommend them to the full House of Representatives for final approval. If the House follows through as expected next week, Mr. Trump could stand trial in the Senate early in the new year.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The Judiciary Committee planned to publicly release text of the articles later on Tuesday. While individual lawmakers will be able to propose amendments to the articles during this week’s debate and potentially force a committee vote on additional charges, they are not expected to substantively change.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Representative Adam B. Schiff of California, the chairman of the Intelligence Committee who oversaw the House’s Ukraine investigation, sought to forcefully dismiss complaints that the House was moving too quickly toward impeachment, a little more than two months after opening their inquiry.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“The argument ‘why don’t you just wait’ amounts to this: Why don’t you just let him cheat in one more election? Why not let him cheat just one more time? Why not let him have foreign help just one more time?”</p></div><aside class="css-ew4tgv" aria-hidden="true"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The Democrats indicated that they would forgo another possible article under discussion in recent weeks that would have charged Mr. Trump with obstruction of justice <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/04/18/us/politics/trump-obstruction-of-justice.html" title="">based on his attempts to thwart</a> Robert S. Mueller III’s investigation into his campaign’s ties to Russian election interference in 2016. That decision reflected a calculated move by Democrats to push forward with a narrow case against Mr. Trump based on his dealings with Ukraine, after some of their moderate lawmakers in conservative-leaning districts signaled they would not support a broader set of charges. </p><div></div><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Though the details differ substantially, the articles of impeachment Democrats outlined on Tuesday echo those the Judiciary Committee approved in 1974 charging President Richard M. Nixon with abuse of power, obstruction of justice and contempt of Congress. Mr. Nixon resigned before the full House had a chance to vote on the articles, amid clear indications that the charges had broad support from members of both parties.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">There is less overlap with the other modern presidential impeachment. In 1998, the House approved impeachment articles charging President Bill Clinton with perjury and obstruction of justice. Two other counts, of perjury and abuse of power, failed in votes on the House floor. It was that kind of split decision that Democratic leaders are determined to avoid this time around. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">With all but a handful of House Republicans firmly united behind Mr. Trump, the charges Democrats have settled on are all but certain to face monolithic Republican opposition. If that does not change, and Mr. Trump continues a defiant defense, the impeachment vote against him could take place strictly along party lines, save for one independent, Representative Justin Amash of Michigan, who has signaled he will join Democrats.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The impeachment effort would also face an uphill battle in the Republican-controlled Senate, where it would take the support of two-thirds of the chamber to convict Mr. Trump and remove him from office — a highly unlikely scenario, particularly in an election year.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">A little more than an hour before Democrats’ announcement, Mr. Trump declared on Twitter that it was “sheer Political Madness” to impeach a president who has done “NOTHING wrong” and overseen “perhaps the strongest economy in our country’s history.” And after it concluded, he wrote on the platform that the Democrats’ charge that he sought Ukraine’s election interference was “ridiculous” and pointed to a statement from Ukraine’s leader that he did not feel pressured to investigate the Bidens.</p></div><aside class="css-ew4tgv" aria-hidden="true"></aside></div><div class="css-cfo9c3"></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The White House press secretary, Stephanie Grisham, accused Democrats of “manufacturing an impeachment inquiry and forcing unfounded accusations down the throats of the American people.” Their goal, she said, was to try to use the House’s impeachment power to weaken Mr. Trump’s chances of re-election.</p></div><aside class="css-ew4tgv" aria-hidden="true"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“The announcement of two baseless articles of impeachment does not hurt the president, it hurts the American people, who expect their elected officials to work on their behalf to strengthen our nation,” Ms. Grisham said in a statement. “The president will address these false charges in the Senate and expects to be fully exonerated, because he did nothing wrong.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Democratic lawyers for the Judiciary Committee and the Intelligence Committee, which carried out the Ukraine inquiry, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/12/09/us/politics/impeachment-trump.html" title="">forcefully argued for the abuse of power and obstruction of Congress charges</a> during a hearing on Monday. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Citing testimony from senior diplomats and White House officials, they accused Mr. Trump and his agents of pressuring Ukraine’s president to announce investigations of former Vice President Joseph R. Biden Jr. and an unsupported claim that Democrats conspired with Ukraine to interfere in the 2016 election. As part of the scheme, they asserted, Mr. Trump withheld a White House meeting and nearly $400 million in security assistance for the country as leverage.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">They also said that Mr. Trump had systematically sought to halt their investigation by ordering government officials not to testify and refusing to hand over documents subpoenaed by the House related to the Ukraine matter. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Republicans pushed back against both conclusions, arguing that Democrats had manufactured a scandal to satiate their hunger to impeach Mr. Trump, a president whose policies they despise. They argued that the evidence gathered by the House had not proved Mr. Trump was acting to benefit himself politically when he pressed Ukraine to announce investigations into his political adversaries. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The decision to forgo a vote on an article of impeachment based on obstruction of justice was not entirely unexpected. House Democrats have debated ever since Mr. Mueller’s report became public last spring whether the behavior detailed — including 10 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/04/18/us/politics/trump-obstruction-of-justice.html" title="">possible instances of obstruction</a> — warranted such action. The issue never unified their caucus in the way the Ukraine allegations have.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Progressive lawmakers including Mr. Nadler pushed repeatedly to include an article on obstruction of justice related in the final impeachment case against Mr. Trump. But the resistance by moderates would have risked splitting the party in a vote on the House floor. </p></div><aside class="css-ew4tgv" aria-hidden="true"></aside></div></section><div></div><div></div><div></div><div></div><div></div><div><div id="bottom-wrapper" class="css-1ede5it"><div id="bottom-slug" introducing two articles of impeachment, charging the president of the United States, Donald J. Trump, with committing high crimes and misdemeanors. The first article is for abuse of power. It is an impeachable offense for the president to exercise the powers of his public office to obtain an improper personal benefit while ignoring or injuring the national interest. That is exactly what President Trump did when he solicited and pressured Ukraine to interfere in our 2020 presidential election, thus damaging our national security, undermining the integrity of the next election, and violating his oath to the American people. These actions moreover are consistent with President Trump’s previous invitations of foreign interference in our 2016 presidential election. And when he was caught, when the House investigated and opened an impeachment inquiry, President Trump engaged in unprecedented, categorical and indiscriminate defiance of the impeachment inquiry. This gives rise to the second article of impeachment, for obstruction of Congress. Here too, we see a familiar pattern in President Trump’s misconduct. A president who declares himself above accountability, above the American people and above Congress’s power of impeachment, which is meant to protect against threats to our democratic institutions, is a president who sees himself as above the law.” “The evidence of the president’s misconduct is overwhelming and uncontested. And how could it not be, when the president’s own words on July 25, ‘I would like you to do us a favor though,’ lay so bare his intentions, his willingness to sacrifice the national security for his own personal interests? And when the president got caught, he committed his second impeachable act, obstruction of Congress, of the very ability to make sure that no one is above the law, not even the president of the United States. The evidence is every bit as strong that President Trump has obstructed Congress fully without precedent and without basis in law. If allowed to stand, it would decimate Congress’s ability to conduct oversight of this president or any other in the future, leaving this president and those who follow to be free to be as corrupt, malfeasant or incompetent as they would like, with no prospect of discovery or
